Democritus had with him a book on madness and melancholy.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> During the 5th century BCE, mental disorders, especially those with <a href="/wiki/Psychosis" title="Psychosis">psychotic</a> traits, were considered <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> in origin,<sup id="cite_ref-Elkes13_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elkes13-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> a view which existed throughout <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Elkes13_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elkes13-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> as well as Egyptian regions.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Religious leaders often turned to versions of <a href="/wiki/Exorcism" title="Exorcism">exorcism</a> to treat mental disorders often utilizing methods that many consider to be cruel or barbaric methods. Trepanning was one of these methods used throughout history.<sup id="cite_ref-Elkes13_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elkes13-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> fostered early studies in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_psychology" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic psychology">Islamic psychology</a> and psychiatry, with many scholars writing about mental disorders. The Persian physician <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi</a>, also known as "Rhazes", wrote texts about psychiatric conditions in the 9th century.<sup id="cite_ref-WaelMohamed_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WaelMohamed-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> As chief physician of a hospital in Baghdad, he was also the director of one of the first psychiatric wards in the world. Two of his works in particular, <i>El-Mansuri</i> and <i>Al-Hawi</i>, provide descriptions and treatments for mental illnesses.<sup id="cite_ref-WaelMohamed_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WaelMohamed-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Abu_Zayd_al-Balkhi" title="Abu Zayd al-Balkhi">Abu Zayd al-Balkhi</a>, was a Persian polymath during the 9th and 10th centuries and one of the first to classify neurotic disorders. He pioneered <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_therapy" title="Cognitive therapy">cognitive therapy</a> in order to treat each of these classified neurotic disorders. He classified neurosis into four <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotional</a> disorders: <a href="/wiki/Fear" title="Fear">fear</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anger" title="Anger">anger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aggression" title="Aggression">aggression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sadness" title="Sadness">sadness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fixation_(psychology)" title="Fixation (psychology)">obsession</a>. Al-Balkhi further classified three types of depression: <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">normal depression</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sadness" title="Sadness">sadness</a> (<i>huzn</i>), <a href="/wiki/Endogeny" class="mw-redirect" title="Endogeny">endogenous</a> depression originating from within the body, and reactive <a href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" class="mw-redirect" title="Clinical depression">clinical depression</a> originating from outside the body.<sup id="cite_ref-Amber_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amber-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Bimaristan" title="Bimaristan">bimaristan</a> was founded in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> in the 9th century, and several others of increasing complexity were created throughout the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a> in the following centuries. Some of the bimaristans contained wards dedicated to the care of mentally ill patients,<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> most of whom suffered from debilitating illnesses or exhibited violence.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Specialist hospitals such as <a href="/wiki/Bethlem_Royal_Hospital" title="Bethlem Royal Hospital">Bethlem Royal Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> were built in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval Europe</a> from the 13th century to treat mental disorders, but were used only as custodial institutions and did not provide any type of treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-Shorter4_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shorter4-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The beginning of psychiatry as a medical specialty is dated to the middle of the nineteenth century,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShorter19971_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShorter19971-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> although its germination can be traced to the late eighteenth century. In the late 17th century, privately run asylums for the insane began to proliferate and expand in size. In 1713 the Bethel Hospital Norwich was opened, the first purpose-built asylum in England.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In 1656, <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV of France</a> created a public system of hospitals for those suffering from mental disorders, but as in England, no real treatment was applied.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShorter19975_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShorter19975-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> attitudes towards the mentally ill began to change. It came to be viewed as a disorder that required compassionate treatment. In 1758 English physician <a href="/wiki/William_Battie" title="William Battie">William Battie</a> wrote his <i>Treatise on Madness</i> on the management of <a href="/wiki/Mental_disorder" title="Mental disorder">mental disorder</a>. It was a critique aimed particularly at the <a href="/wiki/Bethlem_Hospital" class="mw-redirect" title="Bethlem Hospital">Bethlem Hospital</a>, where a conservative regime continued to use barbaric custodial treatment. Battie argued for a tailored management of patients entailing cleanliness, good food, fresh air, and distraction from friends and family. He argued that mental disorder originated from dysfunction of the material brain and body rather than the internal workings of the mind.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShorter19979_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShorter19979-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Philippe_Pinel_%C3%A0_la_Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Philippe_Pinel_%C3%A0_la_Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re.jpg/220px-Philippe_Pinel_%C3%A0_la_Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Philippe_Pinel_%C3%A0_la_Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re.jpg/330px-Philippe_Pinel_%C3%A0_la_Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Philippe_Pinel_%C3%A0_la_Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re.jpg/440px-Philippe_Pinel_%C3%A0_la_Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="869" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Philippe_Pinel_%C3%A0_la_Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dr. Philippe Pinel at the Salpêtrière, 1795 by <a href="/wiki/Tony_Robert-Fleury" title="Tony Robert-Fleury">Tony Robert-Fleury</a>. Pinel ordering the removal of chains from patients at the Paris Asylum for insane women.</div></div></div>
<p>The introduction of moral treatment was initiated independently by the French doctor <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Pinel" title="Philippe Pinel">Philippe Pinel</a> and the English <a href="/wiki/Quaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaker">Quaker</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Tuke" title="William Tuke">William Tuke</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Elkes13_99-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elkes13-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In 1792 Pinel became the chief physician at the <a href="/wiki/Bic%C3%AAtre_Hospital" title="Bicêtre Hospital">Bicêtre Hospital</a>. Patients were allowed to move freely about the hospital grounds, and eventually dark dungeons were replaced with sunny, well-ventilated rooms. Pinel's student and successor, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Esquirol" class="mw-redirect" title="Jean Esquirol">Jean Esquirol</a> (1772–1840), went on to help establish 10 new mental hospitals that operated on the same principles.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Tuke, Pinel and others had tried to do away with physical restraint, it remained widespread into the 19th century. At the <a href="/wiki/The_Lawn,_Lincoln" title="The Lawn, Lincoln">Lincoln Asylum</a> in England, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gardiner_Hill" title="Robert Gardiner Hill">Robert Gardiner Hill</a>, with the support of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Parker_Charlesworth" title="Edward Parker Charlesworth">Edward Parker Charlesworth</a>, pioneered a mode of treatment that suited "all types" of patients, so that mechanical restraints and coercion could be dispensed with — a situation he finally achieved in 1838. In 1839 Sergeant John Adams and Dr. <a href="/wiki/John_Conolly" title="John Conolly">John Conolly</a> were impressed by the work of Hill, and introduced the method into their <a href="/wiki/St_Bernard%27s_Hospital,_Hanwell" title="St Bernard&#39;s Hospital, Hanwell">Hanwell Asylum</a>, by then the largest in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Suzuki_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suzuki-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taom_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taom-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The modern era of institutionalized provision for the care of the mentally ill, began in the early 19th century with a large state-led effort. In England, the <a href="/wiki/Lunacy_Act_1845" title="Lunacy Act 1845">Lunacy Act 1845</a> was an important landmark in the treatment of the mentally ill, as it explicitly changed the status of <a href="/wiki/Mental_illness" class="mw-redirect" title="Mental illness">mentally ill</a> people to <a href="/wiki/Patients" class="mw-redirect" title="Patients">patients</a> who required treatment. All asylums were required to have written regulations and to have a resident qualified <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wright,_1999_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright,_1999-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (November 2014)">full citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 1838, France enacted a law to regulate both the admissions into asylums and asylum services across the country.
In the United States, the erection of state asylums began with the first law for the creation of one in New York, passed in 1842. The <a href="/wiki/Utica_State_Hospital" class="mw-redirect" title="Utica State Hospital">Utica State Hospital</a> was opened approximately in 1850. Many state hospitals in the United States were built in the 1850s and 1860s on the <a href="/wiki/Kirkbride_Plan" title="Kirkbride Plan">Kirkbride Plan</a>, an architectural style meant to have curative effect.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>At the turn of the century, England and France combined had only a few hundred individuals in asylums.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShorter199734_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShorter199734-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> By the late 1890s and early 1900s, this number had risen to the hundreds of thousands. However, the idea that mental illness could be ameliorated through institutionalization ran into difficulties.<sup id="cite_ref-Shorter46_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shorter46-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Psychiatrists were pressured by an ever-increasing patient population,<sup id="cite_ref-Shorter46_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shorter46-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> and asylums again became almost indistinguishable from custodial institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothman_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothman-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1800s, psychiatry made advances in the diagnosis of mental illness by broadening the category of mental disease to include <a href="/wiki/Mood_disorder" title="Mood disorder">mood disorders</a>, in addition to disease level <a href="/wiki/Delusion" title="Delusion">delusion</a> or ir<a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WCFCD_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCFCD-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The 20th century introduced a new psychiatry into the world, with different perspectives of looking at mental disorders. For Emil Kraepelin, the initial ideas behind biological psychiatry, stating that the different mental disorders are all biological in nature, evolved into a new concept of "nerves", and psychiatry became a rough approximation of neurology and neuropsychiatry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShorter1997114_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShorter1997114-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Following <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>'s pioneering work, ideas stemming from <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalytic_theory" title="Psychoanalytic theory">psychoanalytic theory</a> also began to take root in psychiatry.<sup id="cite_ref-Shorter145_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shorter145-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The psychoanalytic theory became popular among psychiatrists because it allowed the patients to be treated in private practices instead of warehoused in asylums.<sup id="cite_ref-Shorter145_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shorter145-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Acetylcholine.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Acetylcholine.svg/220px-Acetylcholine.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="89" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Acetylcholine.svg/330px-Acetylcholine.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Acetylcholine.svg/440px-Acetylcholine.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="208" data-file-height="84" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Acetylcholine.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Otto_Loewi" title="Otto Loewi">Otto Loewi</a>'s work led to the identification of the first neurotransmitter, <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine" title="Acetylcholine">acetylcholine</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>By the 1970s, however, the psychoanalytic school of thought became marginalized within the field.<sup id="cite_ref-Shorter145_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shorter145-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Biological psychiatry reemerged during this time. <a href="/wiki/Psychopharmacology" title="Psychopharmacology">Psychopharmacology</a> became an integral part of psychiatry starting with <a href="/wiki/Otto_Loewi" title="Otto Loewi">Otto Loewi</a>'s discovery of the neuromodulatory properties of <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine" title="Acetylcholine">acetylcholine</a>; thus identifying it as the first-known neurotransmitter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShorter1997246_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShorter1997246-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neuroimaging" title="Neuroimaging">Neuroimaging</a> was first utilized as a tool for psychiatry in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShorter1997270_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShorter1997270-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The discovery of <a href="/wiki/Chlorpromazine" title="Chlorpromazine">chlorpromazine</a>'s effectiveness in treating <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a> in 1952 revolutionized treatment of the disorder,<sup id="cite_ref-Turner2007_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2007-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> as did <a href="/wiki/Lithium_carbonate" title="Lithium carbonate">lithium carbonate</a>'s ability to stabilize mood highs and lows in <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">bipolar disorder</a> in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Psychotherapy was still utilized, but as a treatment for psychosocial issues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShorter1997239_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShorter1997239-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1963, <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">US president</a> <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> introduced legislation delegating the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Mental_Health" title="National Institute of Mental Health">National Institute of Mental Health</a> to administer Community Mental Health Centers for those being discharged from state psychiatric hospitals.<sup id="cite_ref-Shorter280_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shorter280-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Later, though, the Community Mental Health Centers focus shifted to providing psychotherapy for those suffering from acute but less serious mental disorders.<sup id="cite_ref-Shorter280_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shorter280-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately there were no arrangements made for actively following and treating severely mentally ill patients who were being discharged from hospitals, resulting in a large population of chronically homeless people suffering from mental illness.<sup id="cite_ref-Shorter280_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shorter280-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy_and_criticism">Controversy and criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Psychiatry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Controversy and criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Controversy_surrounding_psychiatry" class="mw-redirect" title="Controversy surrounding psychiatry">Controversy surrounding psychiatry</a></div>
<p>Controversy has often surrounded psychiatry, and the term <a href="/wiki/Anti-psychiatry" title="Anti-psychiatry">anti-psychiatry</a> was coined by psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/David_Cooper_(psychiatrist)" title="David Cooper (psychiatrist)">David Cooper</a> in 1967 and was later made popular by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Szasz" title="Thomas Szasz">Thomas Szasz</a>. The basic premise of anti-psychiatry is that: psychiatrists attempt to maliciously classify "normal" people as "deviant;" psychiatric treatments are ultimately more damaging than helpful to patients; and psychiatry's history involves (what may now be seen as) dangerous treatments, such as the frontal lobectomy (commonly called, a <a href="/wiki/Lobotomy" title="Lobotomy">lobotomy</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Several former patient groups have been formed often referring to themselves as "<a href="/wiki/Psychiatric_survivors_movement" title="Psychiatric survivors movement">survivors</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> French literary critic <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a> has performed histories of psychiatry<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> which are foundational to <a href="/wiki/Critical_theory" title="Critical theory">critical theory</a>.
</p>
